        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="cc61856e22f84843969d497b5b553f18">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'b333696d-47bd-49cc-a6d0-381242b103ff'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

class="icon-multi-figure"></i><h2>Figures</h2></a></li><li role="presentation" class="tab__nav__item"><a id="pane-pcw-referencescon-cloned" href="#pane-pcw-references" data-slide-clone="self" aria-controls="pane-pcw-references" role="menuitem" data-slide-target="#pane-pcw-references" class="tab__nav__item__link article-row-right__tabLinks w-slide__btn references-tab cloned-with-ids data-slide-clone="self" aria-controls="pane-pcw-related" role="menuitem" data-slide-target="#pane-pcw-related" class="tab__nav__item__link article-row-right__tabLinks w-slide__btn related-tab    <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d168468758" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>High energy density and flexible electrodes, which have high mechanical properties and electrochemical stability, are critical to the development of wearable electronics. In this work, a free-standing MXene bonded SnS<sub>2</sub> composited nitrogen-doped carbon fibers (MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs)&nbsp;film is reported as a flexible anode for sodium-ion batteries. SnS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles with high-capacity properties are covalently decorated in bio-derived nitrogen-doped 1D carbon fibers (SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs) and further assembled with highly conductive MXene sheets. The addition of bacterial cellulose (BC) can further improve the flexibility of the film. The unique 3D structure of points, lines, and planes can not only offset the disadvantage of low conductivity of SnS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles but also expand the distance between MXene sheets, which is conducive to the penetration of electrolytes. More importantly, the MXene sheets and N-doped 1D carbon fibers (NCFs) can accommodate the large volume expansion of SnS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and trap polysulfide during the cycle. The MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film exhibits better sodium storage and excellent rate performance compared to the SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs. The in situ XRD and ex situ (XRD, XPS, and HRTEM) techniques are used to analyze the sodiation process and to deeply study the reaction mechanism of the films. Finally, the quasi-solid-state full cells with MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>@carbon cloth (NVP@CC) fully demonstrate the application potential of the flexible electrodes.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202305530-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202305530-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In recent years, flexible wearable devices (e.g., human motion detection devices) have been widely studied for their promising applications in portable mobile electronic devices.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0001_R_d168468750e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - J. Semicond. J. Semicond.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To power these flexible electronic devices and accommodate their flexibility, there is an urgent need to develop reliable power supplies. Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have lower costs and higher safety than lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0002_R_d168468750e458" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, developing flexible electrodes with good mechanical properties for high safety and flexible SIBs is valuable for flexible wearable devices. Flexible electrodes are usually deposited or coated with active materials on a flexible current collector (e.g., nickel foam), which makes it impossible to avoid the use of binders, acetylene black, and other additives. Moreover, the flexible current collector increases the electrode mass, making it difficult to fabricate high-energy-density batteries. Therefore, designing lightweight and flexible electrodes with strong mechanical properties and electrochemical stability is vital to achieving high-energy flexible batteries.</p>
         
         <p>MXenes are emerging 2D nanomaterials with high electrical conductivity, abundant terminal groups, large specific surface area, and hydrophilicity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0003_R_d168468750e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Sci.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MXene-based composites have been extensively studied for energy storage as electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0004_R_d168468750e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Eng. J. Small Adv. Mater">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The abundant terminal groups and unique lamellar structure of MXene are beneficial to preparing electrodes by vacuum-assisted filtration strategy and show a potential candidate for flexible electrodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0005_R_d168468750e476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Sci.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the weak van der Waals forces between adjacent few-layer MXene sheets lead to aggregation and self-restacking, severely reducing the electrolyte contact area, and limiting the accessibility of electrolyte ions and the full utilization of MXene active sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0005_R_d168468750e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Sci.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Great efforts have been made to overcome this drawback. Notably, MXene as a flexible and conductive binder to encapsulate carbon material offers a strategy to restrain self-stacking. For example, activated carbon particles, and nitrogen-rich porous carbon nanosheets were introduced into the interlayer of MXene sheets to construct a 3D conductive network, which expands the distance between MXene sheets and accelerates the infiltration of electrolytes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0006_R_d168468750e489" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ACS Energy Lett. J. Semicond.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Different from the above carbon materials, biomass-derived carbon has the advantages of low cost, high yield, and simple synthesis conditions. Aspergillus Niger is a species of fungus with biomass adsorption and can be carbonized to high-quality N-doped 1D carbon, which can be used to prevent the self-restacking of MXene sheets.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0007_R_d168468750e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Energy Chem. Appl. Surf. Sci.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the weak interactions between MXene sheets and nanomaterials lead to a significant reduction in the flexibility and mechanical properties of the hybrid films, which makes them very fragile. Bacterial cellulose (BC) is a low-cost and renewable nanofiber that can be prepared from natural substances<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0008_R_d168468750e499" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in an environmentally friendly manner. Due to its high aspect ratio and 1D nanofiber structure, it can combine with adjacent MXene without significantly reducing the total electrical conductivity,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0009_R_d168468750e505" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> making it a promising candidate for the development of electrode materials with excellent mechanical properties and high electrical conductivity.</p>
         
         <p>Inspired by the aforementioned possibilities, we propose a perfect strategy to construct a free-standing high-conductivity film electrode. Specifically, the <i>Aspergillus niger-</i>derived N-doped carbon fibers with SnS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles are limited between the MXene sheets (MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs) through the vacuum-assisted filtration strategy. Besides, the addition of BC further improves the flexibility of the film (MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC), making it a promising electrode for flexible batteries. More importantly, the design can effectively prevent the self-restacking of MXene and provide sufficient space for sodium ions to embed/de-embed and support the large volume expansion of the active material during the cycle. Thanks to the multi-dimensional structure, as a flexible anode for sodium-ion batteries, the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs electrode exhibits good cycling stability (591.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles with 79% capacity retention) and excellent rate performance (347.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>). In addition, the films with good mechanical properties still retain a capacity of 81.2% after 300 cycles in the quasi-solid-state full cell.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202305530-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202305530-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305530-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202305530-sec-0030-title">2.1 Synthesis and Characterization</h3>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202305530-fig-0001">1a</a> shows the synthesis of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film. Briefly, the few layers of MXene colloidal solution were obtained from the MAX phase using HCl/LiF etching and ultrasonic centrifugal treatment. Besides, the 1D SnS<sub>2</sub> composited N-doped carbon fibers (SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs) were synthesized via the biosorption function of <i>A. niger</i>, carbothermal reduction, and gas phase vulcanization. After that, free-standing films were prepared using the mixture of SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs and MXene colloidal solution by vacuum-assisted filtration. The microstructure and morphology of SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information. The diameter of SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs is about 2&nbsp;µm and the surface is folded (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information). From the EDS mapping of SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information, and the N, S, and Sn are uniformly distributed along the 1D carbon fibers, indicating that the SnS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles are completely embedded in the carbon fibers. In addition, MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC film with excellent mechanical properties was synthesized (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2d</a>, Supporting Information). The MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film exhibit high strain (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a</a>, Supporting Information), while MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC has excellent toughness (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3b</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4b</a>, Supporting Information, the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC film is fully folded and can be restored to its initial state. The detailed synthesis procedures of materials are described in the Experimental Section.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202305530-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e305cfde-acdf-49db-be94-6c784ad69eef/smll202305530-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e305cfde-acdf-49db-be94-6c784ad69eef/smll202305530-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/69a8848e-8ffd-48c0-87e8-4a4e58719a7a/smll202305530-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e305cfde-acdf-49db-be94-6c784ad69eef/smll202305530-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305530-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305530" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the fabrication of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs. b) Cross-sectional SEM image and c) HRTEM image of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs. d) EDS mapping images of Ti, Sn, and S for MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs. e) XRD pattern, f) Raman spectra, and g) survey spectrum of samples.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The cross-sectional morphology of the flexible MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0001">1b</a>. It can be observed that the SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs are encapsulated between the MXene sheets. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a,b</a>, Supporting Information, the SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs facilitates the delamination of MXene in the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film. The self-stacking problem of MXene is solved. Additionally, the calculated BET-specific surface areas of MXene and MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs were 18.735 and 51.672 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. For both MXene and MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs, the overall shapes of the adsorption–desorption hysteresis loops are H3-type (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a,b</a>, Supporting Information). Compared to MXene, MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs shows a slight increase in pore volume for pore sizes of 5 and 6&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5c,d</a>, Supporting Information), with an increase in average pore size from 7.134 to 7.683&nbsp;nm (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the addition of SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs enables the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs to have a large number of micropores (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The porous interconnection structure can facilitate the infiltration of electrolytes and contribute to the formation of stable SEI layers during cycling. High-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0001">1c</a>) clearly shows lattice fringes of 1.3 and 0.589&nbsp;nm, which are attributed to the (002) face of MXene and the (001) face of SnS<sub>2</sub>, respectively. Nearly transparent MXene sheets are observed in TEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0001">1d</a>), showing ultrathin lamellar structures, confirming the structural details that SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs sandwiched between the MXene sheets. Elemental mapping images of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0001">1d</a>) demonstrate the distribution of Ti, Sn, and S in MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film. The selective area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7a,b</a>, Supporting Information) shows typical hexagonal symmetry, additionally proving the presence of SnS<sub>2</sub>. In addition, the films with 40% MXene content exhibit excellent electronic conductivity of about 1.35 E+06 S m<sup>−1</sup> (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). As a result, this unique structure based on point, line, and surface realizes the highly tight conductive integration of SnS<sub>2</sub>, NCFs, and MXene.</p>
            
            <p>XRD, XPS, and Raman measurements were performed to investigate the chemical composition and crystal structure of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film. The XRD pattern of films is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0001">1e</a>, four peaks at 15, 32.1, 41.9, and 50° are indexed to the (001), (101), (102), and (110) crystal planes of SnS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS 23–0677), respectively. In addition, we found that the (002) peak of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs slightly shifts to a lower angle, indicating a slight widening of the interlayer spacing of the MXene sheet due to the intercalation of SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs and BC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0001">1e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0008_R_d168468750e725" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Sci.">8</a>, <a href="#smll202305530-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0010_R_d168468750e725" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Eng. J.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0001">1f</a>, four main peaks at 200, 255, 400, and 620 cm<sup>−1</sup> in the Raman spectrum of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene are associated with titanium carbide and Ti, C, and surface functional groups (-O and -OH) of MXene.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0011_R_d168468750e743" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Eng. J. J. Semicond.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak at 310 cm<sup>−1</sup> in the spectrum of SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs corresponds to the A<sub>1g</sub> vibrational mode of SnS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0012_R_d168468750e757" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Small">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the A<sub>1g</sub> peak of SnS<sub>2</sub> at 310 cm<sup>−1</sup> is not observed in the spectra of the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film because most of the SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs are sandwiched between MXene sheets. The peak of TiO<sub>2</sub> was observed at 150 cm<sup>−1</sup> after assembly with SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs, attributed to the oxidation of the MXene plates during the assembly process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0013_R_d168468750e780" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nat. Commun.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The broad peaks at 1350 and 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup> correspond to sp3 hybridization (D-band) and sp2 hybridization (G-band) of carbon, respectively. Similarly, the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC film exhibits the same chemical composition and crystal structure as MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs. In addition, the chemical composition of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film was investigated at different depths by <i>Z</i>-scan Raman spectroscopy (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), the spectrum at <i>Z</i> = 0&nbsp;µm corresponds to the top surface of the film.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0014_R_d168468750e801" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Semicond.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> With increasing depth (decreasing <i>Z-</i>value), the A<sub>1g</sub> peak of SnS<sub>2</sub> at 310 cm<sup>−1</sup> becomes more and more pronounced.</p>
            
            <p>The XPS spectra of pure MXene film are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information. For pure MXene films, the peaks at 456.02 and 462.32&nbsp;eV correspond to Ti 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ti 2p<sub>1/2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0015_R_d168468750e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Eng. J.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and the energy difference of nearly 6&nbsp;eV between the two asymmetric Ti 2p components provides unequivocal evidence for Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene films.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0016_R_d168468750e838" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0001">1g</a>, the survey spectrum of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film confirms the presence of Ti, C, N, O, S, and Sn elements in the composite. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a</a>, Supporting Information, the Ti 2p spectra were fitted with six distinct peaks at 455.4/459.4, 456.7/461.7, and 458.6/464.5eV, corresponding to Ti-C 2p<sub>3/2</sub> /Ti-C 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, Ti-O-C 2p<sub>3/2</sub>/ Ti-O-C 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, and Ti-O 2p<sub>3/2</sub> /Ti-O 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0017_R_d168468750e865" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Ti–O bond stems from TiO<sub>2</sub> due to oxidization in air. The high-resolution C1s, Sn 3d, and S 2p spectra of samples are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b–d</a>, Supporting Information. For the C 1s spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b</a>, Supporting Information), the C–C and C–S/C–O bonds are located at 284.8 and 286.0<sup>€</sup>eV.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0007_R_d168468750e881" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - J. Energy Chem. Appl. Surf. Sci.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10b,d</a>, Supporting Information, the C–S bonds are derived from SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs. In addition, a C–Ti bond located at 282.0&nbsp;eV is also observed in the composited films.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0018_R_d168468750e892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Eng. J.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Sn 3d spectra are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10c</a>, Supporting Information. The peaks at 487.1 and 495.5&nbsp;eV are ascribed to Sn 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Sn 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, suggesting the existence of Sn<sup>4+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0019_R_d168468750e907" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The S 2p spectra are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10d</a>, Supporting Information, where the peak at 162.0 and 163.8&nbsp;eV corresponds to S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of S<sup>2-</sup>, the peak at 163.6 and 164.8&nbsp;eV corresponds to S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p1/2 of C–S–C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0020_R_d168468750e924" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Appl. Surf. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The XPS results indicated that SnS<sub>2</sub> was successfully synthesized and that SnS<sub>2</sub> may be chemically bonded to NCF via C–S bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0019_R_d168468750e934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs were formed by the physical assembly of SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs and MXene.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305530-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202305530-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance and Mechanism Analysis</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Due to the superior mechanical properties of MXene composited films, they can be directly used as free-standing working electrodes for sodium-ion batteries. The sodium storage capacity of films was investigated in coin-type 2032 cells using sodium metal as the counter electrode. The electrochemical reaction of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs free-standing electrode was assessed by cyclic voltammetry (CV) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202305530-fig-0002">2a</a>). At a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> from 0.01 to 3&nbsp;V, three reduction peaks are observed in the cathode scan of the first cycle. The reduction peak at the higher voltage of around 1.7 and the reduction peak around 0.75&nbsp;V corresponds to the conversion reaction of SnS<sub>2</sub> and the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI). The reduction peak around 0.01&nbsp;V is due to the sodium storage of 1D carbon fibers. Three distinct reduction peaks and a sharp oxidation peak are observed in the subsequent cycles. The reduction peak at the higher voltage of around 1.7 and 1.2&nbsp;V corresponds to the conversion Reaction (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202305530-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>) and the reduction peak around 0.5&nbsp;V corresponds to the alloying Reaction (<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202305530-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a>). The sharp oxidation peak at 1.9&nbsp;V corresponds to the inverse of the conversion reaction. In addition, a weak oxidation peak around 1.3&nbsp;V can be attributed to the inverse reaction of the alloying reaction, that is, the de-alloying of Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>Sn. The CV curves overlap well during the cycle, indicating excellent reversibility of the electrodes.

               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202305530-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d53cef86-c155-48b1-8ea9-f61b675dc704/smll202305530-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202305530:smll202305530-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smll202305530-math-0001.png">

               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202305530-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/94dc347f-2c6b-463d-b336-a2cdd822218d/smll202305530-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202305530:smll202305530-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/smll202305530-math-0002.png">

                              <mspace width="0.16em"></mspace>
                              <mspace width="0.16em"></mspace>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>Na</mi>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">x</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mi>Sn</mi>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{Sn + xN}}{{\mathrm{a}}}^{\mathrm{ + }}{\mathrm{ + x}}{{\mathrm{e}}}^ - \to \,\,{\mathrm{Na}}_{\mathrm{x}}{\mathrm{Sn}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>The electrochemical performance of the MXene film electrode is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information. Due to the tight stacking of MXene layers, Na<sup>+</sup> is difficult to be embedded into MXene. At 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> current density, the reversible capacity of the MXene electrode is lower than 2.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. So the specific capacity of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs and MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC are calculated by the mass of SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs and illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0002">2</a>. The charging/discharging curves of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs electrode at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0002">2b</a>. The initial discharge/charge specific capacity is 1064.9/739.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with an initial Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 69.4%. The low CE and capacity loss during the initial cycle were caused by the decomposition of the electrolyte and the formation of SEI. The CE is higher than 99% in the subsequent cycles, indicating stable cycling performance. After 100 cycles, a reversible capacity of 591.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is still retained (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0002">2c</a>). The rate performance of the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film was further evaluated at current densities from 0.1 to 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0002">2d</a>. The reversible specific capacities of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs are 734.8, 668.7, 647.8, 609.2, 572.1, 490.0, and 431.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> under 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Even at a current density of 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs electrode still shows a specific capacity of 347.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The conspicuous rate performance of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs electrode is superior to those previously reported SnS<sub>2</sub>-based anode for sodium-ion batteries (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202305530-fig-0003">3d,e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0021_R_d168468750e1122" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small">21</a>-<a href="#smll202305530-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0027_R_d168468750e1122" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nano Lett.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The excellent rate performance is attributed to the low charge transfer resistance due to the tight combination of SnS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles and carbon fibers as well as MXene sheets. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0002">2f</a> shows the long-term cycling performance. After 1000 cycles at a high current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs electrode achieves a specific capacity of 435.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and a capacity retention of 71.3%. For SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs, a specific capacity of 46.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is obtained, the rapid decrease of capacity is attributed to the severe volume effect, that the volume of SnS<sub>2</sub> particles expands during cycling, and the NCFs cannot provide sufficient support. In addition, the cycling performance of films with different MXene content was investigated. The film with 40% MXene content shows good specific capacity and cycling stability (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). When a small amount of BC is added to the composite electrode, the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC electrode still shows excellent cyclic stability with a slight capacity attenuation.</div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202305530-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/480eda83-ee10-4e60-bf81-1bdf7ac0b223/smll202305530-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/480eda83-ee10-4e60-bf81-1bdf7ac0b223/smll202305530-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/63c074f8-4ae3-40ae-a63d-85d8167ea82b/smll202305530-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/480eda83-ee10-4e60-bf81-1bdf7ac0b223/smll202305530-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305530-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305530" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs. b) The charge–discharge curves of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Cycling performance and d) rate performance of the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC, MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs, and SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs. e) Comparison of rate capability of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs with other reported. f) Cycling performance at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202305530-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0b5348c1-89de-4260-ab76-f194c9d5cae8/smll202305530-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0b5348c1-89de-4260-ab76-f194c9d5cae8/smll202305530-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a6c781b9-2b4a-480a-9776-1e278cb16eb9/smll202305530-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0b5348c1-89de-4260-ab76-f194c9d5cae8/smll202305530-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305530-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305530" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) In situ XRD during the first discharge-charge and second discharge curves. c–f) HRTEM and SAED images of the film after first discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Meanwhile, the current density and specific capacity calculated by the total mass of the films are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13c</a>, Supporting Information, the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film has a discharge/charge specific capacity of 593/419.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the first cycle at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> with an initial CE of 67.86%. The MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC film has a discharge/charge specific capacity of 594/402 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the first cycle at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, with an initial CE of 67.19%. After 100 cycles, the Capacity retentions of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs and MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC still reached 90.27% and 90%, respectively. Compared to SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCF and MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC, the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs electrode exhibits the highest specific capacity at all current densities (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13c–e</a>, Supporting Information). As a result, the sodium storage performance of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs and MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC is better than that of SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs.</p>
            
            <p>In situ XRD analysis was performed to further elucidate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanisms of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0003">3a,b</a> shows the in situ XRD patterns of the initial discharge/charge and the second discharge of the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs electrode. The details of the structural evolution during cycling are monitored by changes in the (101) peak of SnS<sub>2</sub> around 32°. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0003">3a</a>, the (101) peak of SnS<sub>2</sub> and Be window are shown around 32° and 38°, respectively. The intensity of the (101) peak (at around 32°) of SnS<sub>2</sub> decreases and then increases from open-circuit voltage (OCV) to full charge, and the intensity of the (200) and (220) diffraction peaks (29°–30° and 38°–39°) of Na<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS 47–1698) increases and then decreases. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0003">3b</a>, the intensity of the (101) diffraction peak of SnS<sub>2</sub> at 32° gradually decreases from the OCV to full discharge, and the intensity of the (111) and (220) diffraction peaks (22°–23° and 38°–39°) of Na<sub>2</sub>S gradually increases. As a result, the conversion reaction of the film can occur normally during the second discharge, and it has good cyclic reversibility. For comparison, ex-in situ XRD analyses (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information) were performed to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage mechanism of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs electrodes at different potentials during the initial discharge and after several cycles. The first discharge curves at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14a</a>, Supporting Information, where the letters from “o” to “g” represent the different discharge stages, and the corresponding ex situ XRD patterns are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14b</a>, Supporting Information. During the initial discharge without embedded Na<sup>+</sup>, the voltage drops rapidly to 2.0&nbsp;V. As the discharge proceeds, there is no change in the diffraction peak of SnS<sub>2</sub> at 2.0–1.6&nbsp;V corresponding to the formation of the SEI film. During the electrode discharge from 1.6 to 1.0&nbsp;V, the (001) diffraction peak of SnS<sub>2</sub> gradually disappeared while the electrode discharged to 0.5&nbsp;V, the (101) peak of SnS<sub>2</sub> completely disappeared, indicating the conversion reaction of SnS<sub>2</sub> occurred at 1.6–0.5&nbsp;V. When the electrode discharged to 0.8&nbsp;V, a new peak was observed near 39° which can be indexed to the (220) crystal plane of Na<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS 47–1698). After the free-standing electrode discharged to 0.01&nbsp;V, the characteristic peaks of the (111) and (200) crystal planes of Na<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS 47–1698) appeared at 23°, 27°. The characteristic peaks corresponding to the (312) crystal plane and the (216) crystal plane of Na<sub>14.79</sub> Sn<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS 30–1250) appear around 33° and 34°. The presence of these reaction products indicates the conversion and alloying reactions of SnS<sub>2</sub>, which is consistent with the CV curves. The XRD patterns of the films at high potentials after 1 and 10 cycles are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information. Compared with the XRD patterns in the OCV state, the diffraction peaks of SnS<sub>2</sub> can still be observed in the XRD patterns of the films after several cycles, but its crystallinity deteriorates with the increase of the number of cycles and some diffraction peaks of other inorganic salts (sodium carbonate, etc.) appear. However, the crystallinity of SnS<sub>2</sub> does not seem to affect the insertion/de-insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> based on the cycling performance tests of the films. In addition, the ex situ HRTEM and SAED images in the fully discharged state demonstrate the formation of conversion reactions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0003">3c–f</a>). These results show that the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs electrode achieves reversible Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/de-insertion.</p>
            
            <p>To illuminate the electrochemical kinetics of films, the CV of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs and MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC were carried out at different scan rates from 0.1 to 2.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> and shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202305530-fig-0004">4a,d</a>. The redox peaks corresponding to the conversion and alloying reactions in the CV curves are still observed, indicating that the films have good ion conduction. To further analyze the detailed charge storage process, logI and logV of different redox peaks are recorded and shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0004">4b,e</a>. The relationship between the scan frequency (<i>v</i>) and the peak current (<i>i</i>) obeys the equation <i>i</i> = av<sup>b</sup>, where a <i>b</i>-value of 0.5 or 1.0 represents a diffusion control or capacitive process respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0028_R_d168468750e1367" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0004">4a,b,d,e</a>, the <i>b</i>-values of the redox peaks of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC films are 0.56 and 0.72, respectively, while the <i>b</i>-values of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film are 0.63 and 0.82, respectively. In addition, the capacitive (k<sub>1</sub>v) and diffusion-controlled (k<sub>2</sub>v<sup>1/2</sup>) contributions are further quantitatively separated by the following equation: <i>i</i> = k<sub>1</sub>v + k<sub>2</sub>v<sup>1/2</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0028_R_d168468750e1399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, capacitive contribution dominates sodium storage with 62.46% and 72.92% (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). The capacitive contribution gradually expands as the scan rate increases from 0.2 to 2.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0004">4c,f</a>). This high pseudocapacitance contribution ensures more and faster Na<sup>+</sup> storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0029_R_d168468750e1417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202305530-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7fff81f8-51f4-462b-9f02-c3a8785bcef1/smll202305530-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7fff81f8-51f4-462b-9f02-c3a8785bcef1/smll202305530-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6916bf75-3e1d-46de-a459-355d76220d9c/smll202305530-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7fff81f8-51f4-462b-9f02-c3a8785bcef1/smll202305530-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305530-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305530" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,d) CV curves at 0.2–2&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, b,e) plots of logI versus logV at different peaks, and c,f) capacity contribution at various scan rates of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC and MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film. g) The EIS curves of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs and MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC after cycles. h) GITT curves and diffusion coefficient D<sub>Na+</sub> of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs and MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">The high capacitance contribution and rate performance are attributable to the 3D conductive structure. Moreover, the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs show the lowest charge transfer resistance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0004">4g</a>). The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was performed to further estimate the Na-ion diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Na+</sub>) at 0.01–3&nbsp;V. The D<sub>Na+</sub> is obtained by the formula.

            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305530-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202305530-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Performance of the Quasi-Solid-State Full Cell</h3>
            
            <p>In order to test the practical application value of the free-standing electrode, a sodium ion quasi-solid-state full cell was assembled using MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs as the anode, NVP@CC as the cathode and PVDF-HFP-NaClO<sub>4</sub> as the electrolyte (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202305530-fig-0005">5a</a>). The electrochemical properties of the NVP@CC cathode and the structure and morphology of the PVDF-HFP polymer film are shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>–<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information. PVDF-HFP-NaClO<sub>4</sub> gel polymer electrolytes provide improved safety and excellent flexibility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0030_R_d168468750e1572" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The charge/discharge curves of the NVP@CC cathode and MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs anode in half-cell are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0005">5b</a>. NVP@CC electrode shows a high voltage plateau of about 3.4&nbsp;V. The corresponding charge/discharge curves at various rates of quasi-solid-state sodium ion full cell are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0005">5c,d</a>, exhibiting high electrochemical reversibility. Notably, a clear discharge plateau is still maintained at different current densities, and a reversible specific capacity of 220 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is achieved when the current density increases to 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0005">5e</a>). When the current density returns to 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacity of the battery returns to 308 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating good reversibility of the cell. Even after 300 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the full cell still provides a reversible specific capacity of 273 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202305530-fig-0005">5f</a>, calculation based on anode mass), illustrating the good performance of Mxene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film in a full cell system. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information, MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs and MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC exhibited similar electrochemical performance in quasi-solid-state full cells. In addition, a flexible pouch cell was assembled using sodium metal as the counter electrode and PVDF-HFP-NaClO<sub>4</sub> as the electrolyte. The fabrication process of the pouch cell is shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information, where the size of the electrode material is about 2.5&nbsp; × 2.5&nbsp;cm. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information, the flexible pouch cell in the bent state can also easily power the 1.5&nbsp;V LEDs. Based tests show that the flexible films exhibit good capacity and long cycle life and are therefore promising for use in flexible electronic devices.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202305530-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7300bdb9-9b24-4544-8f37-d6571abeab33/smll202305530-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7300bdb9-9b24-4544-8f37-d6571abeab33/smll202305530-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ed8efa32-e804-4666-9d3c-24892046759a/smll202305530-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7300bdb9-9b24-4544-8f37-d6571abeab33/smll202305530-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202305530-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202305530" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic diagram of the coin-type quasi-solid-state full cell NVP@CC//PVDF-HFP-NaClO<sub>4</sub>//Mxene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs. b) Discharge/charge curves of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs and NVP@CC in half-cells. c) Discharge/charge curves of quasi-solid-state full cell at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Charge/discharge curves and e) Rate performance of quasi-solid-state full cell at different current densities. f) Cycling performance of the quasi-solid-state full cell at a current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202305530-sec-0060">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202305530-sec-0060-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, free-standing films with high electrical conductivity and stability were easily fabricated as flexible anodes for sodium-ion batteries by vacuum-assisted filtration strategy with MXene as the binder, conductive additives, and current collector. At the same time, the MXene sheets alleviate the large volume expansion of the active material during the charging/discharging process. In addition, N-doped carbon fibers and MXene sheets can reduce the dissolution of polysulfides to achieve excellent electrochemical properties. The 3D conducting network and the N-doped carbon improve the electronic conductivity of films. As a result, the MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film as anodes for sodium ion half-cells shows long-term stability at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 100 cycles. Finally, we added bacterial cellulose to improve the mechanical properties of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film, making them promising for use in flexible batteries. The MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film provides a high discharge capacity of 273&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> in a quasi-solid-state sodium ion full cell. This work provides a promising strategy for constructing flexible electrodes for the practical application of flexible batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202305530-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202305530-sec-0070-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305530-sec-0080">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305530-sec-0090">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305530-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202305530-sec-0100-title"> Synthesis of SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs</h4>
                  
                  <p>The <i>A. niger</i> was cultured by the reported method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0031_R_d168468750e1698" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SnCl<sub>4</sub> 5H<sub>2</sub>O (21&nbsp;g) and anhydrous ethanol (300&nbsp;ml) were dissolved in a 500&nbsp;ml conical flask. The prepared <i>A niger</i> was transferred into the solution and incubated for 36 h with shaking. The product was filtered off the solution and then freeze-dried at −80 °C under vacuum. The dried precursors were pyrolyzed at 600 °C (3 °C min<sup>−1</sup>) under argon for 2 h (named Sn@NCFs). Sn@NCFs and sublimated sulfur (1:4) were calcined at 400 °C for 4 h in Ar to prepare SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs composites.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305530-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202305530-sec-0110-title"> Synthesis of the Few-Layers Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene</h4>
                  
                  <p>First, 4.8&nbsp;g of LiF was added to a 9&nbsp;M L<sup>−1</sup> solution of hydrochloric acid (60&nbsp;ml) and stirred for 1&nbsp;h. After that, 3&nbsp;g of Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> MAX was slowly added and etched with magnetic stirring at 35 °C for 48 h. The etching product was collected by centrifugation and stirred magnetically for 1 h in 2&nbsp;M L<sup>−1</sup> sulfuric acid solution (60&nbsp;ml). Finally, the precipitate was washed with deionized water by shaking and centrifugation at 3500&nbsp;rpm. The colloidal solution of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene (concentration of about 10&nbsp;mg ml<sup>−1</sup>) was obtained from the supernatant.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305530-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202305530-sec-0120-title"> Synthesis of MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs and MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC Films</h4>
                  
                  <p>SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs composites were added to deionized water (30&nbsp;ml) and sonicated for 2&nbsp;h to obtain smaller particle sizes of SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs. After that, they were added to a colloidal solution of MXene (10&nbsp;mg ml<sup>−1</sup>), stirred for 12&nbsp;h, and then vacuum-filtered. The films contain 40% MXene and 60% SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs. For comparison, the films with 60% and 65% MXene were also prepared. The films were dried naturally at 25 °C under an air atmosphere. The MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs-BC film was prepared in a similar process with BC (0.8%).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305530-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202305530-sec-0130-title"> Synthesis of NVP@CC</h4>
                  
                  <p>The carbon cloth was heated in concentrated nitric acid at 100 °C for 2 h. The slurry, including (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, acetylene black, and polyvinylidene fluoride with a mass ratio of 7:2:1, was coated on the carbon cloth and vacuum dried at 110 °C for 12&nbsp;h.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305530-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202305530-sec-0140-title"> Synthesis of Sodium Ion Conducting Gel Polymer</h4>
                  
                  <p>The PVDF-HFP films were synthesized in a slightly different way from previous work.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202305530-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202305530-bib-0032_R_d168468750e1797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Solid State Ionics">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> First, 1.6&nbsp;g of PVDF-HFP (Mw = 455 000, Sigma-Aldrich) pellets were dissolved in 6.0&nbsp;g N, N-dimethylformamide (DMF, solvent) with 0.2&nbsp;g deionized water (non-solvent and pore producer). The gel was flatted on a PTFE sheet and dried at 40 °C for 2 days. Then, the dried white PVDF-HFP films were put into a electrolyte (1&nbsp;mol L<sup>−1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in vinyl carbonate (EC):diethyl carbonate (DEC) = 1:1 containing 5 vol% fluorinated vinyl carbonate) in a glove box for 24&nbsp;h.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305530-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202305530-sec-0150-title"> Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The scanning electron microscope (SEM, Magellan 400) and transmission electron microscope (TEM, JOEL JSM-2010F) were performed to characterize the morphology and microstructure. The XRD patterns and Raman spectra were collected by the powder diffractometer (Rigaku, SmartLab SE) and Renishaw inVia (532&nbsp;nm laser). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) (VG ESCALAB MK II) was used to analyze the chemical states of samples. The conductivity of the samples was tested by a four-point probe conductivity instrument (YAOS, FM100GH). The resistance of the films was characterized semi-quantitatively by a multimeter (Fluke 101). Specific surface area and pore size distribution were obtained using the BET test (JWGB INSTRUMENTS, JW-BK100C).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305530-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202305530-sec-0160-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The CHI760E workstation and LAND instrument were used to test electrochemical performance. The coin cells were fabricated using a separator (Whatman GF/D) and sodium foil. The electrolyte was 1.0&nbsp;M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC), diethyl carbonate (DEC) (1:1), and 5% fluoroethylene carbonate. Other electrodes were prepared by mixing a slurry of 7:2:1 by weight of an active substance, acetylene black, and polyvinylidene fluoride in N-methylpyrrolidone, coating it on Cu foil, and drying it under vacuum at 110 °C for 12 h.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202305530-sec-0170">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202305530-sec-0170-title"> Assembly and Testing of Quasi-Solid-State Full Cells</h4>
                  
                  <p>Quasi-solid-state full cells were assembled by using MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs film and NVP@CC with pre-embedded sodium as the anode and cathode, respectively, and sodium ion-containing gel polymer as the quasi-solid-state electrolyte and separator. The weight ratio of NVP@CC and MXene/SnS<sub>2</sub>@NCFs is about 3:1 according to the capacity balance principle. The quasi-solid-state cells were assembled into CR2032-type coin cells for electrochemical testing on the LAND instrument.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202305530-sec-0180-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
         
         <p>M.W. and D.L. contributed equally to this work. The authors sincerely acknowledge financial support from the National Natural Science Foundation of China (NSFC Grant Nos. 22293041 and 52372193).</p>
         
         <ol></ol>
